<p><b>Dara Grace Torres</b> (born April 15, 1967) is an American former competitive <a href="/wiki/Swimming_(sport)" title="Swimming (sport)">swimmer</a> who is a twelve-time Olympic medalist and former world record-holder in three events. Torres is the first swimmer to represent the United States in five <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> (1984, 1988, 1992, 2000 and 2008), and, at age 41, the oldest swimmer to earn a place on the U.S. Olympic team. At the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a>, she competed in the 50-meter <a href="/wiki/Freestyle_swimming" title="Freestyle swimming">freestyle</a>, 4×100-meter <a href="/wiki/Medley_swimming" title="Medley swimming">medley relay</a>, and 4×100-meter freestyle relay, and won <a href="/wiki/Silver_medal" title="Silver medal">silver medals</a> in all three events.</p>
<p>Torres has won twelve Olympic medals (four gold, four silver, four bronze), one of three women with the most Olympic women's swimming medals. The others are fellow Americans <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Thompson" title="Jenny Thompson">Jenny Thompson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Coughlin" title="Natalie Coughlin">Natalie Coughlin</a>. Torres won five medals at the <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Olympics" title="2000 Summer Olympics">2000 Summer Olympics</a> when, at age 33, she was the oldest member of the 2000 U.S. Olympic Swim Team. She has also won at least one medal in each of the five Olympics in which she has competed, making her one of only a handful of Olympians to earn medals in five different Games.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Torres was born to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban-American" title="Cuban-American">Cuban-American</a> family, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>, the daughter of Edward Torres and Marylu Kauder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-downey8162008_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-downey8162008-1">[1]</a></sup> Her father was a real estate developer and casino owner, originally from <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>; her mother Marylu was a former <a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">American</a> model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-downey8162008_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-downey8162008-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> Torres grew up in <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">Beverly Hills, California</a>, the fifth of six children and the older of two girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> As a 7-year-old, she followed in the footsteps of her older brothers by joining their community <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y.M.C.A." title="Y.M.C.A.">Y.M.C.A.</a> for swimming practice; afterward, she signed up for the swimming club in Culver City to train.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> At 14, she won the national open championship in the 50-yard freestyle by defeating the then-current champion, <a href="/wiki/Jill_Sterkel" title="Jill Sterkel">Jill Sterkel</a>, a college junior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin6181984_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-levin6181984-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>She attended the Westlake School for Girls (now <a href="/wiki/Harvard-Westlake_School" title="Harvard-Westlake School">Harvard-Westlake School</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-downey8162008_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-downey8162008-1">[1]</a></sup> and competed for the Westlake swim team under coach Darlene Bible from the seventh grade through her sophomore year in high school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white3152013_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-white3152013-4">[4]</a></sup> She was also a member of the Westlake basketball, gymnastics and volleyball teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white3152013_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-white3152013-4">[4]</a></sup> During her 1983–84 high school junior year, she left home to swim for the <a href="/wiki/Mission_Viejo_Nadadores" title="Mission Viejo Nadadores">Mission Viejo Nadadores</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mission_Viejo,_California" title="Mission Viejo, California">Mission Viejo, California</a>, while training for her first Olympics under coach <a href="/wiki/Mark_Schubert" title="Mark Schubert">Mark Schubert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin6181984_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-levin6181984-3">[3]</a></sup> After the 1984 Olympics, Torres returned to the Westlake School to graduate in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white3152013_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-white3152013-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="College_swimming_career">College swimming career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: College swimming career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Torres accepted an athletic scholarship to attend the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> in Gainesville, Florida, where she swam for the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Gators_swimming_and_diving" title="Florida Gators swimming and diving">Florida Gators swimming and diving</a> team in <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> (NCAA) competition under coach <a href="/wiki/Randy_Reese" title="Randy Reese">Randy Reese</a> from 1986 to 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufmediasupp_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ufmediasupp-5">[5]</a></sup> In her four years as a Gator swimmer, Torres won nine <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> (SEC) individual championships, including the 50-yard freestyle (1987, 1988, 1989), 100-yard freestyle (1987, 1988, 1989), 200-yard freestyle (1987), and 100-yard butterfly (1988, 1989); she was also a member of twelve of the Gators' SEC championship relay teams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufmediasupp_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-ufmediasupp-5">[5]</a></sup> Torres won three NCAA individual national championships (50-yard freestyle, 100-yard freestyle, 100-yard butterfly) in 1988; and was a member of six of the Gators' NCAA championship relay teams, including the 400-yard freestyle relay in 1986; the 200-yard and 400-yard medley relays, and the 400-yard freestyle relay in 1988; and the 200-yard and 400-yard medley relays in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufmediasupp_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-ufmediasupp-5">[5]</a></sup> She was named the SEC Athlete of the Year in 1988, SEC Female Swimmer of the Year in 1987 and 1989, and earned twenty-eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-American" title="All-American">All-American</a> swimming honors—the maximum number possible during a college career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufmediasupp_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-ufmediasupp-5">[5]</a></sup> Torres also lettered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_Gators_volleyball" title="Florida Gators volleyball">volleyball</a> at Florida, playing the sport in her fifth year after having exhausted her NCAA eligibility in swimming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncaa11142013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncaa11142013-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Torres graduated from the university with a bachelor's degree in telecommunications in 1990, and was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/List_of_University_of_Florida_Athletic_Hall_of_Fame_members" title="List of University of Florida Athletic Hall of Fame members">University of Florida Athletic Hall of Fame</a> as a "Gator Great" in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In November 2013, she was named as a recipient of the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Silver_Anniversary_Awards" title="Silver Anniversary Awards">NCAA Silver Anniversary Award</a>, presented annually to six distinguished former student-athletes on the 25th anniversary of their final school year of athletic eligibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncaa11142013_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncaa11142013-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_swimming_career">International swimming career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: International swimming career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1984.2C_1988_and_1992_Olympics">1984, 1988 and 1992 Olympics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1984, 1988 and 1992 Olympics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">1984 Summer Olympics</a> in Los Angeles, Torres was a member of the winning U.S. women's <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_1984_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_freestyle_relay" title="Swimming at the 1984 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre freestyle relay">4×100-meter freestyle relay</a> team, swimming in the first-round qualifying heat and earning a gold medal in the event final.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup> Her winning teammates in that final included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nancy_Hogshead" title="Nancy Hogshead">Nancy Hogshead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jenna_Johnson" title="Jenna Johnson">Jenna Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Steinseifer" title="Carrie Steinseifer">Carrie Steinseifer</a>; <a href="/wiki/Jill_Sterkel" title="Jill Sterkel">Jill Sterkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wayte" title="Mary Wayte">Mary Wayte</a> also swam in the event's second-round qualifying heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Olympics" title="1988 Summer Olympics">1988 Summer Olympics</a> in Seoul, South Korea, Torres qualified for the U.S. Olympic women's team in one individual event and two relay events. Torres earned a bronze medal for swimming for the third-place U.S. women's team in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_1988_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_freestyle_relay" title="Swimming at the 1988 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre freestyle relay">4×100-meter freestyle relay</a> final, together with teammates <a href="/wiki/Mitzi_Kremer" title="Mitzi Kremer">Mitzi Kremer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Walker" title="Laura Walker">Laura Walker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wayte" title="Mary Wayte">Mary Wayte</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> She also earned a silver medal for swimming the freestyle leg of the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_1988_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_medley_relay" title="Swimming at the 1988 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre medley relay">4×100-meter medley relay</a> for the second-place U.S. team in the preliminary heats, but not the final.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup> Individually, Torres also placed seventh in the final of the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_1988_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_100_metre_freestyle" title="Swimming at the 1988 Summer Olympics – Women's 100 metre freestyle">100-meter freestyle</a> event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Torres qualified for the U.S. Olympic women's team in a single event for the <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics" title="1992 Summer Olympics">1992 Summer Olympics</a> in Barcelona. She swam the second leg of the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_1992_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_freestyle_relay" title="Swimming at the 1992 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre freestyle relay">4×100-meter freestyle relay</a> for the winning U.S. team that included <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Haislett" title="Nicole Haislett">Nicole Haislett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angel_Martino" title="Angel Martino">Angel Martino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Thompson" title="Jenny Thompson">Jenny Thompson</a>, and earned a gold medal for her efforts in the event final and first-round qualifying heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_comeback:_2000_Olympics">First comeback: 2000 Olympics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First comeback: 2000 Olympics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After seven years out of competitive swimming, Torres began to train for an Olympic comeback in 1999 under the guidance of coach <a href="/wiki/Richard_Quick" title="Richard Quick">Richard Quick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid7202008_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-reid7202008-13">[13]</a></sup> She won five medals at the <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Olympics" title="2000 Summer Olympics">2000 Summer Olympics</a> in Sydney, but saved her best for two of the major relay events of the U.S. Olympic women's team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sro2000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-sro2000-14">[14]</a></sup> She swam the second leg for the winning U.S. women's team in final of the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_freestyle_relay" title="Swimming at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre freestyle relay">4×100-meter freestyle relay</a> that included <a href="/wiki/Amy_Van_Dyken" title="Amy Van Dyken">Amy Van Dyken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Courtney_Shealy" title="Courtney Shealy">Courtney Shealy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Thompson" title="Jenny Thompson">Jenny Thompson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sro2000_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-sro2000-14">[14]</a></sup> Torres won a second gold medal for anchoring the winning U.S. team in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_medley_relay" title="Swimming at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre medley relay">4×100-meter medley relay</a>, together with teammates B.J. Bedford, <a href="/wiki/Megan_Jendrick" title="Megan Jendrick">Megan Quann</a> and Jenny Thompson in the final.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sro2000_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-sro2000-14">[14]</a></sup> Individually, Torres also earned bronze medals – her first Olympic medals in individual events – in each of the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_50_metre_freestyle" title="Swimming at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's 50 metre freestyle">50-meter freestyle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_100_metre_butterfly" title="Swimming at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's 100 metre butterfly">100-meter butterfly</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2000_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_100_metre_freestyle" title="Swimming at the 2000 Summer Olympics – Women's 100 metre freestyle">100-meter freestyle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sro2000_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-sro2000-14">[14]</a></sup> tying teammate and rival Jenny Thompson for third place in the last event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> At 33 years old, Torres became the oldest woman to win an Olympic medal in swimming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson8182006_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson8182006-17">[17]</a></sup> She was the oldest member of the U.S. Olympic swim team, but won more medals (5) than any other U.S. team member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_comeback:_2008_Olympics">Second comeback: 2008 Olympics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Second comeback: 2008 Olympics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dara_Torres.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Dara_Torres.jpg/220px-Dara_Torres.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Dara_Torres.jpg/330px-Dara_Torres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Dara_Torres.jpg/440px-Dara_Torres.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dara_Torres.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Torres at the Missouri Grand Prix in 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On August 1, 2007, at age 40 and just 16 months after giving birth to her first child, Torres won the 100-meter freestyle at the U.S. Nationals in Indianapolis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> On August 4, she set a new American record in the 50-meter freestyle of 24.53 seconds, breaking her own record of 24.63 seconds set at the 2000 Summer Olympics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> She lowered her initial American record by 1.62 seconds. Torres has broken or lowered her own American record in the 50-meter freestyle ten times, which is the most by any American swimmer in any event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_Olympic_Trials_(swimming)" title="2008 United States Olympic Trials (swimming)">2008 U.S. Olympic Trials</a>, Torres returned to the pool and qualified for a spot in her fifth Olympic Games at the age of 41, a first for an American female swimmer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scott8182008_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-scott8182008-23">[23]</a></sup> She became the oldest U.S. Olympic swimmer in history and the first American swimmer to appear in five Olympic Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid7202008_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-reid7202008-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the trials, she qualified for the event finals in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_50_metre_freestyle" title="Swimming at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's 50 metre freestyle">50-meter freestyle</a> and broke the American record with a time of 24.38 seconds in the semifinal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alfonsi7082008_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-alfonsi7082008-24">[24]</a></sup> In the 50-meter finals, she broke that record for the ninth time, setting it at 24.25 seconds and winning the top American women's spot in the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Torres also qualified in the 100-meter freestyle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> but later withdrew from the individual 100-meter freestyle event to focus her efforts on the 50-meter freestyle and 4×100-meter relay events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robb7082008_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-robb7082008-27">[27]</a></sup> On July 30, at the U.S. swim team's final training in Singapore, Torres, <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Beard" title="Amanda Beard">Amanda Beard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Coughlin" title="Natalie Coughlin">Natalie Coughlin</a> were elected captains of the U.S. Olympic women's swimming team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to pre-empt any speculation that she might be taking performance-enhancing drugs, Torres volunteered for a new enhanced drug-testing program by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="United States Anti-Doping Agency">U.S. Anti-Doping Agency</a> (USADA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> "I want to be an open book", she said. "I want people to know that I'm 41, and I'm doing this right. I'm clean."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson7142008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson7142008-30">[30]</a></sup> In addition to employing her training team of coach Michael Lohberg, a sprint coach, a chiropractor and two masseuses, Torres used resistance stretching trainers, calling it her "secret weapon" for continued success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid7202008_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-reid7202008-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crouse11182007_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-crouse11182007-31">[31]</a></sup> Her $100,000-per-year training costs were partially subsidized by her corporate sponsors <a href="/wiki/Speedo" title="Speedo">Speedo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg L.P.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid7202008_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-reid7202008-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>"I think this performance ranks up there with the biggest performances in sports ever. . . . It puts Dara in the ranks of Michael Phelps, Michael Jordan, Tiger Woods. What she has done is really not measurable." Michael Lohberg, Torres' coach, discussing her winning three Olympic silver medals at the age of 41.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a> in Beijing, Torres won a silver medal as the anchor swimmer of the second-place U.S. team in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_freestyle_relay" title="Swimming at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre freestyle relay">4×100-meter freestyle relay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup> It was the fifth time in five tries she earned an Olympic medal in the 4×100-meter freestyle relay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup> With the American relay team's second-place finish, she became the oldest swimmer to win a medal in Olympic history, surpassing British swimmer <a href="/wiki/William_Robinson_(swimmer)" title="William Robinson (swimmer)">William Robinson</a>, who was 38 at the time of the 1908 Summer Olympics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 17, 2008, she won the silver medal in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_50_metre_freestyle" title="Swimming at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's 50 metre freestyle">50-meter freestyle</a>, finishing in a new American record time of 24.07 seconds, one one-hundredth (0.01) of a second behind the winner, <a href="/wiki/Britta_Steffen" title="Britta Steffen">Britta Steffen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carey8162008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-carey8162008-34">[34]</a></sup> Her second-place time was a new American record, and 0.18 of a second faster than she swam at the U.S. Olympic Trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carey8162008_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-carey8162008-34">[34]</a></sup> Thirty-five minutes later, she won another silver medal swimming the freestyle anchor leg for the second-place U.S. team in the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_4_%C3%97_100_metre_medley_relay" title="Swimming at the 2008 Summer Olympics – Women's 4 × 100 metre medley relay">4×100-meter medley relay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson8252008_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson8252008-35">[35]</a></sup> Her split on the 4×100 medley relay (52.27 seconds) was the fastest 100-meter freestyle split in relay history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson8252008_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-anderson8252008-35">[35]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_records_in_swimming#Women" title="United States records in swimming">American record</a> for the women's 100-meter freestyle as an individual event was 53.39 seconds as of August 2008, making Torres' time more than a full second faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson8252008_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-anderson8252008-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Torres' twelve Olympic medals tied the all-time medal record for a female Olympic swimmer set by fellow American <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Thompson" title="Jenny Thompson">Jenny Thompson</a> in 2004; American <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Coughlin" title="Natalie Coughlin">Natalie Coughlin</a> subsequently equaled the record in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap7292012_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap7292012-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath_and_retirement">Aftermath and retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Aftermath and retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the U.S. National Championships in 2009, Torres won the 50-meter freestyle with the fourth-best time in the world for the year (24.42), and she also placed in the 50-meter butterfly, qualifying her to compete in those events at the <a href="/wiki/2009_World_Aquatics_Championships" title="2009 World Aquatics Championships">2009 World Championships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This was the first time since 1986 that Torres competed in the World Championships; she placed eighth in the 50-meter freestyle and she did not advance beyond the qualifying heats in the 50-meter butterfly.</p>
<p>Following reconstructive surgery of one of her knees, she began training with the goal of competing in the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/2012_United_States_Olympic_Trials_(swimming)" title="2012 United States Olympic Trials (swimming)">2012 United States Olympic Trials</a>, she placed fourth in the finals of the 50-meter freestyle, 0.32 of a second behind the winner, <a href="/wiki/Jessica_Hardy" title="Jessica Hardy">Jessica Hardy</a>, and 0.09 of a second behind the second qualifier, <a href="/wiki/Kara_Lynn_Joyce" title="Kara Lynn Joyce">Kara Lynn Joyce</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shipley7022012_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-shipley7022012-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap7032012_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap7032012-40">[40]</a></sup> Only the top two finishers in each trials event qualified for the 2012 U.S. Olympic Team, and as a result, Torres concluded her Olympic career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shipley7022012_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-shipley7022012-39">[39]</a></sup> After the 2012 Trials, Torres announced her retirement from competitive swimming, concluding her career with 12 Olympic medals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap7032012_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-ap7032012-40">[40]</a></sup> Her Olympic career spanned twenty-four years from 1984 to 2008 and five Olympic Games (1984, 1988, 1992, 2000, 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sroprofile_9-9"><a href="#cite_note-sroprofile-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_outside_swimming">Life outside swimming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Life outside swimming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="480" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg/170px-Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg/255px-Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg/340px-Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dara_Torres_in_2010_Heart_Truth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Torres in the 2010 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_Truth" title="Heart Truth">Heart Truth</a> fashion show</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Torres has worked in television as a reporter and announcer for American networks <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turner_Network_Television" title="Turner Network Television">TNT</a>, <a href="/wiki/OLN" title="OLN">OLN</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_News_Channel" title="Fox News Channel">Fox News Channel</a>, and hosted the golf show <i>The Clubhouse</i> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resort_Sports_Network" title="Resort Sports Network">Resort Sports Network</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saslow8022007_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-saslow8022007-41">[41]</a></sup> She has also worked as a model, and was the first elite swimmer to model swimwear in the <i>Sports Illustrated</i> <a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated_Swimsuit_Issue" title="Sports Illustrated Swimsuit Issue">Swimsuit Issue</a>, appearing in the 1994 edition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crouse11182007_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-crouse11182007-31">[31]</a></sup> She was elected to the <a href="/wiki/International_Jewish_Sports_Hall_of_Fame" title="International Jewish Sports Hall of Fame">International Jewish Sports Hall of Fame</a> in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>After 1992, she married and subsequently divorced sports producer Jeff Gowen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> After 2000, she <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">converted to Judaism</a> before marrying the Israeli surgeon Itzhak Shasha (her own father had been Jewish).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> Torres and Shasha later divorced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weil6292008_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-weil6292008-2">[2]</a></sup> Torres and reproductive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocrinologist" title="Endocrinologist">endocrinologist</a> David Hoffman began dating after her break-up with Shasha, and they became the parents of Tessa Grace Torres-Hoffman, born in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reid7202008_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-reid7202008-13">[13]</a></sup> After the birth of their daughter, Hoffman, a masters swimmer, persuaded her to begin training again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robb7082008_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-robb7082008-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Torres is a veteran celebrity swimmer for <a href="/wiki/Swim_Across_America" title="Swim Across America">Swim Across America</a>, a charitable organization that raises funds for cancer research in which she has participated for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saaprofile_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-saaprofile-43">[43]</a></sup> She is the author of two books: <i>Age is Just a Number: Achieve Your Dreams at Any Stage in Your Life</i> (with Elizabeth Weil), and <i>Gold Medal Fitness: A Revolutionary 5-Week Program</i> (with Billie Fitzpatrick).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_records">World records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dara_Torres&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: World records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Women's 50-meter freestyle</b></p>
